       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Information_leverage&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Information leverage (page does not exist)">
        information leverage
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       concerns over giving the government mechanisms to control the use of all power using activities
      </li>
      <li>
       concerns over RF emissions from smart meters
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Security">
       Security
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Security">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      With the advent of cybercrime there is also concern on the security of the infrastructure, primarily that involving communications technology. Concerns chiefly center around the communications technology at the heart of the smart grid. Designed to allow real-time contact between utilities and meters in customers' homes and businesses, there is a risk that these capabilities could be exploited for criminal or even terrorist actions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookSG_7-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-bookSG-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      One of the key capabilities of this connectivity is the ability to remotely switch off power supplies, enabling utilities to quickly and easily cease or modify supplies to customers who default on payment. This is undoubtedly a massive boon for energy providers, but also raises some significant security issues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cybercriminals have infiltrated the U.S. electric grid before on numerous occasions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aside from computer infiltration, there are also concerns that computer
      <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">
       malware
      </a>
      like
      <a href="/wiki/Stuxnet" title="Stuxnet">
       Stuxnet
      </a>
      , which targeted SCADA systems which are widely used in industry, could be used to attack a smart grid network.
     </p>
     <p>
      Electricity theft is a concern in the U.S. where the smart meters being deployed use the RF technology of Fastrak transponders to communicate with the electricity transmission network.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      People with knowledge of electronics can devise interference devices to cause the smart meter to report lower than actual usage.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Similarly, the same technology can be employed to make it appear that the energy the consumer is using is being used by another customer, increasing their bill.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_challenges_to_adoption">
       Other challenges to adoption
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other challenges to adoption">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Before a utility installs an advanced metering system, or any type of
      <a href="/wiki/Smart_system" title="Smart system">
       smart system
      </a>
      , it must make a business case for the investment. Some components, like the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Power_system_stabilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Power system stabilizer (page does not exist)">
       power system stabilizers
      </a>
      (PSS)
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2011)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      installed on generators are very expensive, require complex integration in the grid's control system, are needed only during emergencies, and are only effective if other suppliers on the network have them. Without any incentive to install them, power suppliers don't.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarado_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Alvarado-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most utilities find it difficult to justify installing a communications infrastructure for a single application (e.g. meter reading). Because of this, a utility must typically identify several applications that will use the same communications infrastructure – for example, reading a meter, monitoring power quality, remote connection and disconnection of customers, enabling demand response, etc. Ideally, the communications infrastructure will not only support near-term applications, but unanticipated applications that will arise in the future. Regulatory or legislative actions can also drive utilities to implement pieces of a smart grid puzzle. Each utility has a unique set of business, regulatory, and legislative drivers that guide its investments. This means that each utility will take a different path to creating their smart grid and that different utilities will create smart grids at different adoption rates.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some features of smart grids draw opposition from industries that currently are, or hope to provide similar services. An example is competition with cable and DSL Internet providers from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_line_communication" title="Power line communication">
       broadband over powerline internet access
      </a>
      . Providers of SCADA control systems for grids have intentionally designed proprietary hardware, protocols and software so that they cannot inter-operate with other systems in order to tie its customers to the vendor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carlson-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Power_Theft_.2F_Power_Loss">
       Power Theft / Power Loss
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Power Theft / Power Loss">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Various "smart grid" systems have dual functions. This includes Advanced Metering Infrastructure systems which, when used with various software can be used to detect power theft and by process of elimination, detect where equipment failures have taken place. These are in addition to their primary functions of eliminating the need for human meter reading and measuring the time-of-use of electricity.
     </p>
     <p>
      The worldwide power loss including theft is estimated at approximately two-hundred billion dollars annually.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Electricity theft also represents a major challenge when providing reliable electrical service in developing countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buevich_95.E2.80.9398_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Buevich_95.E2.80.9398-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Deployments_and_attempted_deployments">
       Deployments and attempted deployments
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Deployments and attempted deployments">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Enel" title="Enel">
        Enel
       </a>
      </b>
      . The earliest, and one of the largest, example of a smart grid is the Italian system installed by Enel S.p.A. of Italy. Completed in 2005, the Telegestore project was highly unusual in the utility world because the company designed and manufactured their own meters, acted as their own system integrator, and developed their own system software. The Telegestore project is widely regarded as the first commercial scale use of smart grid technology to the home, and delivers annual savings of 500 million euro at a project cost of 2.1 billion euro.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NETL01_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NETL01-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a class="external text" href="https://www.smartgrid.gov/" rel="nofollow">
        US Dept. of Energy - ARRA Smart Grid Project
       </a>
       :
      </b>
      One of the largest deployment programs in the world to-date is the U.S. Dept. of Energy's Smart Grid Program funded by the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009. This program required matching funding from individual utilities. A total of over $9 billion in Public/Private funds were invested as part of this program. Technologies included Advanced Metering Infrastructure, including over 65 million Advanced "Smart" Meters, Customer Interface Systems, Distribution &amp; Substation Automation, Volt/VAR Optimization Systems, over 1,000 Synchrophasors, Dynamic Line Rating, Cyber Security Projects, Advanced Distribution Management Systems, Energy Storage Systems, and Renewable Energy Integration Projects. This program consisted of Investment Grants (matching), Demonstration Projects, Consumer Acceptance Studies, and Workforce Education Programs. Reports from all individual utility programs as well as overall impact reports will be completed by the second quarter of 2015.
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Austin, Texas
      </b>
      . In the US, the city of
      <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">
       Austin, Texas
      </a>
      has been working on building its smart grid since 2003, when its utility first replaced 1/3 of its manual meters with smart meters that communicate via a wireless
      <a href="/wiki/Mesh_networking" title="Mesh networking">
       mesh network
      </a>
      . It currently manages 200,000 devices real-time (smart meters, smart thermostats, and sensors across its service area), and expects to be supporting 500,000 devices real-time in 2009 servicing 1 million consumers and 43,000 businesses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">
        Boulder, Colorado
       </a>
      </b>
      completed the first phase of its smart grid project in August 2008. Both systems use the smart meter as a gateway to the
      <a href="/wiki/Home_automation" title="Home automation">
       home automation
      </a>
      network (HAN) that controls smart sockets and devices. Some HAN designers favor decoupling control functions from the meter, out of concern of future mismatches with new standards and technologies available from the fast moving business segment of home electronic devices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Hydro_One" title="Hydro One">
        Hydro One
       </a>
      </b>
      , in
      <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
       Ontario
      </a>
      , Canada is in the midst of a large-scale Smart Grid initiative, deploying a standards-compliant communications infrastructure from Trilliant. By the end of 2010, the system will serve 1.3 million customers in the province of Ontario. The initiative won the "Best AMR Initiative in North America" award from the Utility Planning Network.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The City of
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">
        Mannheim
       </a>
      </b>
      in Germany is using realtime
      <a href="/wiki/Broadband_over_power_lines" title="Broadband over power lines">
       Broadband Powerline (BPL)
      </a>
      communications in its Model City Mannheim "MoMa" project.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">
        Adelaide
       </a>
      </b>
      in Australia also plans to implement a localised green Smart Grid electricity network in the Tonsley Park redevelopment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">
        Sydney
       </a>
      </b>
      also in Australia, in partnership with the Australian Government implemented the Smart Grid, Smart City program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%89vora" title="Évora">
        Évora
       </a>
      </b>
      . InovGrid is an innovative project in
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%89vora" title="Évora">
       Évora
      </a>
      , Portugal that aims to equip the electricity grid with information and devices to automate grid management, improve service quality, reduce operating costs, promote energy efficiency and environmental sustainability, and increase the penetration of renewable energies and electric vehicles. It will be possible to control and manage the state of the entire electricity distribution grid at any given instant, allowing suppliers and energy services companies to use this technological platform to offer consumers information and added-value energy products and services. This project to install an intelligent energy grid places Portugal and
      <a href="/wiki/Energias_de_Portugal" title="Energias de Portugal">
       EDP
      </a>
      at the cutting edge of technological innovation and service provision in Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       E-Energy
      </b>
      - In the so-called
      <b>
       E-Energy
      </b>
      projects several German utilities are creating first nucleolus in six independent model regions. A technology competition identified this model regions to carry out research and development activities with the main objective to create an "Internet of Energy."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Massachusetts
      </b>
      . One of the first attempted deployments of "smart grid" technologies in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      was rejected in 2009 by electricity
      <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">
       regulators
      </a>
      in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Massachusetts" title="Commonwealth of Massachusetts">
       Commonwealth of Massachusetts
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_(United_States)" title="State (United States)">
       US state
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to an article in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Globe" title="Boston Globe">
       Boston Globe
      </a>
      , Northeast Utilities'
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Massachusetts_Electric_Company" title="Western Massachusetts Electric Company">
       Western Massachusetts Electric Co.
      </a>
      subsidiary actually attempted to create a "smart grid" program using public subsidies that would switch
      <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">
       low income
      </a>
      customers from post-pay to pre-pay billing (using "
      <a href="/wiki/Smart_card" title="Smart card">
       smart cards
      </a>
      ") in addition to special hiked "premium" rates for electricity used above a predetermined amount.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This plan was rejected by regulators as it "eroded important
      <a href="/wiki/Consumer_protection" title="Consumer protection">
       protections
      </a>
      for
      <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">
       low-income
      </a>
      customers against shutoffs".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid_80-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Globe" title="Boston Globe">
       Boston Globe
      </a>
      , the plan "
      <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">
       unfairly targeted
      </a>
      low-income customers and circumvented Massachusetts laws meant to help
      <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">
       struggling consumers
      </a>
      keep the lights on".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid_80-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A spokesman for an
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">
       environmental group
      </a>
      supportive of smart grid plans and Western Massachusetts' Electric's aforementioned "smart grid" plan, in particular, stated "If used properly, smart grid technology has a lot of potential for reducing peak demand, which would allow us to shut down some of the oldest, dirtiest power plants... It’s a tool."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid_80-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeWMECOSmartGrid-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       eEnergy Vermont
      </b>
      consortium
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      is a US statewide initiative in
      <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">
       Vermont
      </a>
      , funded in part through the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act_of_2009" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009">
       American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009
      </a>
      , in which all of the electric utilities in the state have rapidly adopted a variety of Smart Grid technologies, including about 90% Advanced Metering Infrastructure deployment, and are presently evaluating a variety of dynamic rate structures.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <b>
       Netherlands
      </b>
      a large-scale project (&gt;5000 connections, &gt;20 partners) was initiated to demonstrate integrated smart grids technologies, services and business cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.factorymicrogrid.com/en/index.aspx" rel="nofollow">
        LIFE Factory Microgrid
       </a>
       (LIFE13 ENV / ES / 000700)
      </b>
      is a demonstrative project that is part of the
      <a href="/wiki/The_LIFE_Programme" title="The LIFE Programme">
       LIFE+ 2013
      </a>
      program (European Commission), whose main objective is to demonstrate, through the implementation of a full-scale industrial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SmartGrids" title="SmartGrids">
       smartgrid
      </a>
      that microgrids can become one of the most suitable solutions for energy generation and management in factories that want to minimize their
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issue" title="Environmental issue">
       environmental impact
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="OpenADR_Implementations">
       OpenADR Implementations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: OpenADR Implementations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Certain deployments utilize the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenADR" title="OpenADR">
       OpenADR
      </a>
      standard for load shedding and demand reduction during higher demand periods.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="China">
       China
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: China">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The smart grid market in China is estimated to be $22.3 billion with a projected growth to $61.4 billion by 2015.
      <a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">
       Honeywell
      </a>
      is developing a demand response pilot and feasibility study for China with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Grid_Corp" title="State Grid Corp">
       State Grid Corp
      </a>
      . of China using the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenADR" title="OpenADR">
       OpenADR
      </a>
      demand response standard. The State Grid Corp., the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Academy_of_Science" title="Chinese Academy of Science">
       Chinese Academy of Science
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      intend to work together to develop standards for China’s smart grid rollout.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">
       United Kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenADR" title="OpenADR">
       OpenADR
      </a>
      standard was demonstrated in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bracknell,_England" title="Bracknell, England">
       Bracknell, England
      </a>
      , where
      <i>
       peak
      </i>
      use in commercial buildings was reduced by 45 percent. As a result of the pilot, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_and_Southern_Energy" title="Scottish and Southern Energy">
       Scottish and Southern Energy
      </a>
      (SSE) said it would connect up to 30 commercial and industrial buildings in Thames Valley, west of
      <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
       London
      </a>
      , to a
      <a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">
       demand response
      </a>
      program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
       United States
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: United States">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 2009, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" title="US Department of Energy">
       US Department of Energy
      </a>
      awarded an $11 million grant to
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_California_Edison" title="Southern California Edison">
       Southern California Edison
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">
       Honeywell
      </a>
      for a
      <a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">
       demand response
      </a>
      program that automatically turns down energy use during peak hours for participating industrial customers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eightyfive_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eightyfive-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Department of Energy awarded an $11.4 million grant to Honeywell to implement the program using the OpenADR standard.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hawaiian Electric Co. (HECO) is implementing a two-year pilot project to test the ability of an ADR program to respond to the intermittence of wind power.
      <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">
       Hawaii
      </a>
      has a goal to obtain 70 percent of its power from renewable sources by 2030. HECO will give customers incentives for reducing power consumption within 10 minutes of a notice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Guidelines.2C_standards_and_user_groups">
       Guidelines, standards and user groups
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Guidelines, standards and user groups">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Part of the
      <a href="/wiki/IEEE_Smart_Grid" title="IEEE Smart Grid">
       IEEE Smart Grid Initiative
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">
       IEEE
      </a>
      2030.2 represents an extension of the work aimed at utility storage systems for transmission and distribution networks. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE_P2030" title="IEEE P2030">
       IEEE P2030
      </a>
      group expects to deliver early 2011 an overarching set of guidelines on smart grid interfaces. The new guidelines will cover areas including batteries and
      <a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">
       supercapacitors
      </a>
      as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">
       flywheels
      </a>
      . The group has also spun out a 2030.1 effort drafting guidelines for integrating
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">
       electric vehicles
      </a>
      into the smart grid.
     </p>
     <p>
      IEC TC57 has created a family of international standards that can be used as part of the smart grid. These standards include IEC61850 which is an architecture for substation automation, and IEC 61970/61968 – the Common Information Model (CIM). The CIM provides for common semantics to be used for turning data into information.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenADR" title="OpenADR">
       OpenADR
      </a>
      is an open-source smart grid communications standard used for demand response applications.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is typically used to send information and signals to cause electrical power-using devices to be turned off during periods of higher demand.
     </p>
     <p>
      MultiSpeak has created a specification that supports distribution functionality of the smart grid. MultiSpeak has a robust set of integration definitions that supports nearly all of the software interfaces necessary for a distribution utility or for the distribution portion of a vertically integrated utility. MultiSpeak integration is defined using extensible markup language (XML) and web services.
     </p>
     <p>
      The IEEE has created a standard to support
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchrophasors" title="Synchrophasors">
       synchrophasors
      </a>
      – C37.118.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The UCA International User Group discusses and supports real world experience of the standards used in smart grids.
     </p>
     <p>
      A utility task group within LonMark International deals with smart grid related issues.
     </p>
     <p>
      There is a growing trend towards the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TCP/IP" title="TCP/IP">
       TCP/IP
      </a>
      technology as a common communication platform for smart meter applications, so that utilities can deploy multiple communication systems, while using IP technology as a common management platform.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE_P2030" title="IEEE P2030">
       IEEE P2030
      </a>
      is an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">
       IEEE
      </a>
      project developing a "Draft Guide for Smart Grid Interoperability of Energy Technology and Information Technology Operation with the Electric Power System (EPS), and End-Use Applications and Loads".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST" title="NIST">
       NIST
      </a>
      has included
      <a href="/wiki/ITU-T" title="ITU-T">
       ITU-T
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/G.hn" title="G.hn">
       G.hn
      </a>
      as one of the "Standards Identified for Implementation" for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smart_Grid" title="Smart Grid">
       Smart Grid
      </a>
      "for which it believed there was strong stakeholder consensus".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      G.hn is standard for high-speed communications over power lines, phone lines and coaxial cables.
     </p>
     <p>
      OASIS EnergyInterop' – An OASIS technical committee developing XML standards for energy interoperation. Its starting point is the California OpenADR standard.
     </p>
     <p>
      Under the Energy Independence and Security Act of 2007 (EISA),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST" title="NIST">
       NIST
      </a>
      is charged with overseeing the identification and selection of hundreds of standards that will be required to implement the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smart_Grid" title="Smart Grid">
       Smart Grid
      </a>
      in the U.S. These standards will be referred by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST" title="NIST">
       NIST
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_Energy_Regulatory_Commission" title="Federal Energy Regulatory Commission">
       Federal Energy Regulatory Commission
      </a>
      (FERC). This work has begun, and the first standards have already been selected for inclusion in NIST's Smart Grid catalog.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, some commentators have suggested that the benefits that could be realized from Smart Grid standardization could be threatened by a growing number of patents that cover Smart Grid architecture and technologies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If patents that cover standardized Smart Grid elements are not revealed until technology is broadly distributed throughout the network ("locked-in"), significant disruption could occur when patent holders seek to collect unanticipated rents from large segments of the market.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Smart_grid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: See also">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

        <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crystal_energy.svg">
          <img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="124" data-file-width="130" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/29px-Crystal_energy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/44px-Crystal_energy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Crystal_energy.svg/59px-Crystal_energy.svg.png 2x" width="29"/>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle">

          Energy portal
         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li style="display:table-row">

           <span class="nowrap">
            2008-12-12
           </span>
          </span>
          .
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASmart+grid&amp;rft.atitle=Wide+Area+Protection+System+for+Stability&amp;rft.date=2008-04-22&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nari-relays.com%2Fen%2Ffiles%2FWide%2520Area%2520Protection%2520System%2520for%2520Stability.pdf&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         Examples are given of two events, one stabilizing the system after a fault on a 1 gigawatt HVDC feed, with response timed in milliseconds.
        </span>
       </li>
